In this, apart from the implicit masdar, the word 'aklan' is explicitly mentioned, and it is a nakirah in a negative context, so it becomes general. When it has generality, specification by intention is possible, meaning if the speaker intends to eat a specific thing, it is valid. If it is said: This is a question about 'la akul' – that when there is no generality in 'la akul', then he should not be guilty of breaking the oath for every individual act of eating, but he is guilty for every type of eating. We say in response: He is guilty for every individual act of eating because the individual act of eating is included under the essence of eating, and the essence of eating was prohibited for him due to the oath. That is, the meaning of 'la akul' is that the essence of eating will not occur from me. The non-existence of the essence of eating depends on the non-existence of any individual act of eating at all. This meaning is by way of implication, because if any individual act of eating occurs, then by that individual the essence is established, whereas the essence was prohibited by the oath, so in that case he breaks the oath. The reason is not that the word 'la akul' indicates all individuals by way of explicit text so that the charge of generality is made and then after generality the possibility of specification is said.

If it is said [in objection] that 'la usakin falan' – this is an objection to 'no generality for implication'. The summary of the objection is that if a person says 'I will not cohabit with so-and-so', this speech indicates a place by way of implication. If the speaker intends a specific single house, then this intention is valid. So this is specification, and specification requires that there was generality before it, because specification is after generality. So generality is established in implication, whereas the rule was that there is no generality for implication. We say: His intention is valid only because 'musakanah' is of two types. The answer is that 'musakanah' is in reality of two types: one is incomplete – that the house is one but each resides in separate rooms – that is the meaning of 'being in one house'. The second is complete – that both live together in one room. So intending one room is not from the topic of generality of implication, but rather from the topic that it is the intention of one of the possible meanings of a homonym, or intending one type of the two types of the genus of 'musakanah'. Since this is not the topic of implication, your objection falls. It will be completed in this section.

And here the author changed the wording of the matn by advancing and delaying. 'Matn' here means 'Tanqih' which is the matn of Tawdih. In the previous version, the next phrase 'mimma yattasil bi dhalik' to 'fayajri fihi al-umum wa al-khusus' and 'wa li dhalik qulna' was advanced, because Fakhr al-Islam's words were like that. But the author in the present version of the matn advanced 'wa li dhalik' and delayed 'mimma yattasil' so that all discussions of umum muqtada and its specification are gathered in the matn. So 'fanawi al-kamil' is connected to the previous phrase – that in 'la usakin' he intended the complete type, so it is valid. 'Wa li dhalik' means because the implication does not have generality, we said that if one says 'Anti taliq' or 'Tallaqtuk' and intends three [divorces], his intention of three is invalid. Because the masdar that is established from the mentioned words of divorce – i.e., al-talaq from the speaker as a creation of divorce – is a legal matter, not a linguistic one. Therefore, its establishment is by implication, and implication does not have generality of individuals, so intending three is not correct. As for the point that this is a legal matter and not a linguistic one, the reason is that the word 'Anti taliq' indicates linguistically that the woman is described with divorce, but does not indicate that the husband creates divorce. Since the woman being described with divorce legally depends on the husband giving her divorce, this matter is legal and is established by implication. So it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and the extent of necessity is one divorce. As for the word 'Tallaqtuk' – although it indicates linguistically the occurrence of divorce from the husband explicitly, but its linguistic indication is on the past masdar, not on the masdar that occurs in the present. It would need to be linguistic, but since he did not give divorce in the past, it is a lie which is useless. However, the Lawgiver, for the sake of correcting speech, considered it as a creation of divorce in the present from the speaker. So this indication is by implication, not linguistic. It is also estimated to the extent of necessity. Contrary to 'Talliqi nafsak' – because it allows the intention of three. Because its meaning is 'perform the act of divorce', so the establishment of the masdar in the future is by way of language, not implication. So it is like an expressed word. Therefore it takes the ruling of an expressed masdar, and it is valid to apply it to the minimum or to the entire genus, and the entire genus of divorce is three, so intending three is correct. Like other nouns of genera – just as in nouns of genera it is permissible to apply to the minimum or to the entire genus, so here it is permissible, because the whole as a whole is a single legal entity. So a noun of genus has a real singular and a legal singular, so intending the legal singular is valid like the real singular. Then it is said: 'thubut al-baynunah' etc. The question is:
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Hanafi usul rule that 'implication (muqtada) does not have general meaning (la umum li al-muqtada)'. The author explains this rule through several examples from oaths and divorce. First, in the case of an oath like 'la akul' (I will not eat), the word 'akl' is a generic noun in a negative context, which would normally give generality. However, the Hanafi position is that the actual meaning of the oath is the negation of the essence of eating, which is implied by the words. This implication (muqtada) does not have generality, so if the speaker intended to abstain from a specific food, that intention is valid. The author responds to an objection that the oath-taker becomes liable for breaking the oath by any act of eating, which seems to assume generality. The answer is that he becomes liable because each individual act includes the essence, which was prohibited. It is not because the words 'la akul' explicitly cover all individuals. Next, the author addresses an objection based on the phrase 'la usakin falan' (I will not cohabit with so-and-so). The objector claims this implies a place (makan) by implication, and if the speaker intends a specific house, that is valid specification, which would require prior generality in the implication. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing two types of cohabitation (musakanah): incomplete (living in different rooms of the same house) and complete (living in one room). Intending a specific room is not about implication but about choosing one of two meanings of the word 'musakanah', which is a homonym. Thus, the rule that implication has no generality remains intact. The passage then discusses divorce phrases: 'Anti taliq' and 'Tallaqtuk' are analyzed. For 'Anti taliq', the indication of the creation of divorce from the husband is by implication (since linguistically it only describes the woman), so it cannot be generalized; thus intending three divorces is invalid, and only one divorce is realized. For 'Tallaqtuk', although it seems to state a past act, it is treated by the Lawgiver as a present creation, so its indication is also by implication, limited to one divorce. In contrast, 'Talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself) linguistically indicates the future act, so the masdar (source noun) is considered explicit, allowing the intention of three because nouns of genera can be applied to the minimum or the entire genus (here three being the entire genus of divorce). The commentator concludes by noting that the author rearranged the matn to keep all discussions of this rule together.

Key Terms
لا عموم للمقتضى (la umum li al-muqtada): The principle that implied meanings (muqtada) do not carry general applicability; they are restricted to the minimum necessary for the utterance to be valid.
مقتضى (muqtada): An implied meaning that is not explicitly stated but is necessary for the utterance to be correct or to match the speaker's intended legal effect.
مساكنة (musakanah): Cohabitation or living together; in this context, it is divided into incomplete (living in separate rooms in the same house) and complete (sharing the same room).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle 'la umum li al-muqtada', if a husband says to his wife 'Anti taliq' and intends three divorces, which of the following is the correct ruling?
A) Three divorces occur because he intended them.
B) Only one divorce occurs because the implication of divorce creation is limited to the minimum.
C) The divorce is invalid because the wording is ambiguous.
D) Two divorces occur as a compromise.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule 'la umum li al-muqtada' restricts implied meanings to the minimum necessary, preventing them from being generalized like explicit speech.

Iqtida' and Niyyah: Why Intention of Three Divorces Fails
Explaining why intention of three divorces fails in some phrases due to iqtida'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that iqtida' (implication by necessity) restricts legal effect to the minimum necessary, and identify which divorce phrases allow intention of multiple divorces.

Core Idea
Iqtida' limits legal effect to the minimum; only explicit linguistic indication can convey intention of multiple divorces.

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary dissects a critical distinction in how divorce pronouncements operate. When a husband says 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) or 'tallaqtuki' (I divorced you), the divorce is established by iqtida'—a shar'i implication, not linguistic literalness. Because iqtida' does not permit generalization (umum), the husband's intention to pronounce three divorces is ineffective. In contrast, phrases like 'tallaqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself) are linguistically explicit and allow such intention. This distinction is vital for both Hanafi fatawa and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate linguistic vs. shar'i indication
In 'anti taliq', analyze that linguistically it only describes the woman as 'divorced'; it does not directly state the act of divorcing. The legal effect (enactment of divorce) is a necessary implication (iqtida') provided by the Shari'ah to validate the speech.
The literal meaning alone would be a lie if no divorce occurred, so the Shari'ah corrects it by implying the enactment.
2
Apply the iqtida' limitation
Hanafi usul holds that iqtida' is restricted to the minimum needed (muqaddar bi qadr al-darurah). Therefore, in 'anti taliq' only one divorce is established; the husband's intention to intend three is irrelevant because iqtida' does not carry umum.
This applies equally to 'tallaqtuki' because the past tense is reinterpreted as present enactment by iqtida'.
3
Contrast with explicit linguistic phrases
Examine 'tallaqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself): the verb is in the imperative, and the masdar (al-talaq) is understood linguistically and explicitly. Such a phrase is treated as uttered speech (manzilat al-malfuz), allowing both the minimum (one) and the whole genus (three) to be intended.
This is analogous to other names of genera where intending the 'hukmi unit' (three as a whole) is valid.
4
Address the 'anti ba'in' exception
Though 'anti ba'in' also uses iqtida', the word 'baynunah' has two types (sughra and kubra). Therefore, intending one of the two is valid; this does not contradict the iqtida' rule because the genus itself has distinct divisions.
In contrast, 'talaq' has no types—only numerical degrees—so iqtida' restricts to one.
5
Distinguish iqtida' from mahdhuf
Mahdhuf (ellipsis) is a type of omission that, when restored, changes the grammatical structure and wording. It is considered linguistically explicit and can carry umum. Example: 'wa is'ali al-qaryah' (ask the village) with 'ahl' omitted. Restoring 'ahl' transforms the object, making it explicit.
Iqtida' does not change the wording; it adds a necessary legal implication without altering syntax.

And he changed here the expression of the matn by advancing and delaying. Thus: 'matn' refers to Tanqih which is the matn of Tawdih. In its previous copy, the earlier expression 'mimma yattasilu bi dhalika' was preceded by 'fayajri fihi al-umum wa al-khusus' and 'wa li-dhalika qulna' because Fakhr al-Islam's expression was like that. But the author in this present copy of the matn made advancement and delay: he advanced 'wa li-dhalika' etc. and delayed 'mimma yattasilu' so that all discussions of umum muqtada and its khusus would come together in the matn. So 'fannawa al-kamil' is connected to the earlier expression: i.e., in 'la yusakin' he intended the complete type, so it is valid. 'Wa li-dhalika' i.e., because there is no umum in muqtada, therefore we said: if in 'anti taliq' and 'tallaqtuki' he intends three, then his intention of three is invalid. Because the source (masdar) that is established from the mentioned words of divorce—i.e., the masdar 'al-talaq' from the speaker as an enactment of divorce—is a shar'i matter, not linguistic. So its establishment is by way of iqtida', and there is no umum of individuals in iqtida', so the intention of three is not valid. As for the remaining point that it is shar'i not linguistic, the reason is that the word 'anti taliq' linguistically indicates that the woman is described with divorce, and does not indicate that the husband has established divorce by way of enactment. Since the woman's being described with divorce is shar'i, dependent on the husband divorcing her, therefore it is established by way of iqtida'. So it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and the extent of necessity is one divorce. And the word 'tallaqtuki' although it explicitly indicates linguistically the establishment of divorcing from the husband, but its linguistic indication is upon the past tense masdar, not upon the masdar that occurs at the present moment. It should be لغو (invalid) because he did not divorce in the past, so it is a lie which is لغو. But the Shari'ah, for the sake of correcting the speech, made it an enactment of divorce at the present moment from the speaker. So this indication is by iqtida' not linguistic. It is also estimated to the extent of necessity. Contrary to 'tallaqi nafsaki' because it includes intention of three. Because its meaning is 'do the act of divorce', so the establishment of the masdar in the future is by way of language, not iqtida'. So it is like an uttered word. So this masdar, in the ruling of an uttered masdar, its application to the least and to the whole genus is valid. And the genus of divorce is three, so intention of three is valid. Like other names of genera, for in names of genera applying to the least and to the whole is permissible. Because the sum as a whole is a hukmi unit, so in a noun of genus there is a real unit and a hukmi unit, so intending the hukmi unit is valid like the real unit. Then if it is said: 'the establishment of baynunah...' the question is that 'anti ba'in' is exactly similar to 'anti taliq'. Just as the establishment of divorce was a shar'i matter, similarly in 'anti ba'in' the establishment of baynunah is a shar'i matter, so this would also be iqtida', therefore intention of three should not be valid just as it was not valid in 'anti taliq'. Yet in 'anti ba'in' intention of three is valid. We say: Yes, your point is correct, but baynunah is of two types: baynunah sughra and baynunah kubra. Therefore whichever of the two is intended is valid. And it is not like talaq, because in talaq there is no difference of types, rather its basis is difference of number. And from that which is connected to this: mahdhuf is also connected and attached to this iqtida'. Mahdhuf is that whose affirmation changes and alters the wording of the speech, contrary to iqtida' al-nass, because its affirmation does not change the wording of the speech. Example: 'wa is'ali al-qaryah' (and ask the village) — this is an example of mahdhuf; the intended meaning is 'ask the people', and the word 'ahl' is omitted here. The affirmation of the word 'ahl' changes the wording because the relation is transferred from the word 'qaryah' to the word 'ahl'. Previously, 'al-qaryah' was object, now 'ahl' becomes object. So the real object is the word 'ahl', so it is established linguistically and its rank is that of an uttered word, and in it umum and khusus apply, which could not apply in muqtada. His statement 'wa li-dhalika' is an explanation and clarification of that 'wa li-dhalika qulna fi anti taliq...' and by mentioning the referent of 'li-dhalika' he said: i.e., for what we mentioned that there is no umum at all in muqtada, therefore the intention of three divorces in 'anti taliq' and 'tallaqtuki' is not valid because the indication of these words to divorce is by way of iqtida', not by way of language. Because linguistically, 'anti taliq' only indicates that the woman is described with divorce, but it does not indicate the establishment of divorce by way of enactment from the speaker. That is a shar'i matter, not established linguistically. Because the woman's being described with divorce is shar'i, dependent on the husband divorcing his wife, so it is established by way of iqtida'. And there is no umum in muqtada; rather it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and the extent of necessity is one divorce. Then if it is said: 'the divorce that is established from the speaker by way of enactment...' the objection is that the divorce that is established from the speaker by way of enactment cannot be established by iqtida', because al-muqtada is a proof for the objection?
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work discussing the principle of iqtida' (implied meaning) and its application to divorce pronouncements. The commentator begins by noting that the author of the matn (Al-Tawdih) rearranged the text to bring together all discussions on umum muqtada and its khusus. The key ruling is that when a husband says 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) or 'tallaqtuki' (I divorced you), his intention to pronounce three divorces is invalid because the indication of divorce in these phrases is by iqtida'—that is, the literal meaning does not establish the legal effect directly; the Shari'ah implies the divorce from the descriptive statement. Since iqtida' does not allow generalization (umum), only one divorce is necessary. In contrast, the phrase 'tallaqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself) is explicit and operates linguistically, so the intention of three is valid. Similarly, 'anti ba'in' (you are irrevocably separate) allows intention of either major or minor separation because baynunah has two types. The passage then defines mahdhuf (ellipsis) as an omission that changes the wording when restored, unlike iqtida' which does not change the wording. The example 'wa is'ali al-qaryah' (ask the village) means ask the people, and restoring 'ahl' changes the grammatical structure, so it is considered linguistically explicit and can carry umum. This distinction is crucial for understanding how legal effects are derived from different types of textual indications.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): An implied meaning necessary for the validity of the statement but not explicit; the legal effect is established by necessity, not literal wording.
محذوف (mahdhuf): An omitted word that, when restored, changes the grammatical structure of the speech; it is considered linguistically explicit.
مقتضى (muqtada): The necessary implication from a text; in this context, the implied divorce that is legally effective but limited to necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the intention of three divorces invalid in 'anti taliq' but valid in 'tallaqi nafsaki'?
A) Because 'anti taliq' is a lie and 'tallaqi nafsaki' is true.
B) Because 'anti taliq' indicates divorce by iqtida' (limited to one), while 'tallaqi nafsaki' is linguistically explicit (allows umum).
C) Because 'tallaqi nafsaki' is in the imperative mood which is stronger.
D) Because 'anti taliq' is a past tense and 'tallaqi nafsaki' is present.

Key Takeaway
Iqtida' restricts legal effect to the minimum necessary and does not allow generalization; only explicit linguistic indication can carry intention of multiple divorces.

Iqtida' and Intention of Triple Divorce in 'Anti Taliq'
Explaining iqtida' and mahzuf, and proving that intention of triple divorce is invalid in iqtida'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish iqtida' from mahzuf and apply the Hanafi rule that iqtida' carries no generality to derive that intention of three divorces in 'Anti taliq' is invalid.

Core Idea
Iqtida' implies only what is necessary for correctness, never more than one divorce in 'Anti taliq'.

This passage deepens your understanding of iqtida' (necessary implication) and mahzuf (ellipsis), two key concepts in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author uses the divorce phrase 'Anti taliq' to demonstrate that when a ruling is established by iqtida', it is limited to the essential requirement—here, a single divorce—and cannot be multiplied by intention. The distinction between iqtida' and mahzuf is critical because mahzuf allows generality, while iqtida' does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction between iqtida' and mahzuf
Read the passage where the author defines mahzuf as an omitted word whose inclusion changes the grammatical structure, unlike iqtida' which does not alter the uttered words. Note the Quranic example 'Wa is'al al-qaryah' (And ask the village) where 'ahl' (people) is omitted.
Mahzuf has the status of uttered words and allows generality; iqtida' does not.
2
Analyze the linguistic meaning of 'Anti taliq'
Linguistically, 'Anti taliq' means 'You are described with divorce'—an informatory statement. For it to be truthful, the woman must actually be divorced. This necessity is supplied by iqtida', implying the husband's act of divorce.
The proof of divorce is by iqtida', not by direct linguistic meaning.
3
Apply the rule: iqtida' has no generality
Since iqtida' only provides what is necessary for correctness, and one divorce suffices to make the statement true, only one divorce is established. Intentions of three are ineffective because iqtida' cannot be extended beyond the essential extent.
This is the core Hanafi ruling.
4
Evaluate the objection and the Hanafi responses
The objection claims that if divorce is established directly, it falls under 'ibarah al-nass (explicit meaning), allowing intention of three. The author gives two responses: (1) The Lawgiver does not abandon linguistic placement; iqtida' remains. (2) Even if metaphor is considered, iqtida' involves no word to specify, so metaphor is invalid.
The second response also notes that the attribute 'being divorced' is singular and cannot be multiplied.
5
Compare with mahzuf to reinforce the distinction
Mahzuf allows generality and particularity because the omitted word is linguistically present. Iqtida' does not. This contrast is essential for understanding when intentions can affect rulings.
Example: In 'Anti ba'in', intention of three is valid because banunah has multiple types.

If you say 'Anti ba'in' (you are irrevocably divorced), it is exactly similar to 'Anti taliq' – just as the proof of divorce is a shar'i matter, similarly in 'Anti ba'in' the proof of irrevocability is a shar'i matter, so this is also iqtida'. Therefore, intention of three should not be valid, just as it was not valid in 'Anti taliq'. Yet in 'Anti ba'in' intention of three is valid. We said: Yes, your point is correct, but banunah (irrevocability) is of two types: minor and major. So whichever one is intended, it is valid. Not so the word talaq, because in it there is no difference of types but rather its basis is difference of number. And connected to this is mahzuf (ellipsis). Mahzuf is that whose affirmation changes and alters the expressed speech, unlike iqtida' of the text, whose affirmation does not alter the expressed speech. For example, 'Wa is'al al-qaryah' (And ask the village). This is an example of mahzuf; the meaning is 'ask the people of the village' and the word 'ahl' is omitted. The affirmation of 'ahl' alters the expressed speech because the attribution transfers from the word 'village' to the word 'people'. Previously, 'al-qaryah' was object, now 'ahl' becomes object. So the real object is 'ahl'; therefore this is established linguistically and has the status of uttered words, and generality and particularity apply to it – which could not apply in iqtida'. His saying 'wa li-dhalika' (and therefore) is an explanation of our saying in the text about 'Anti taliq'. Mentioning the referent of 'li-dhalika', he said: i.e., because of what we mentioned – that in iqtida' there is no generality at all. Therefore, in 'Anti taliq' and 'Tallaqtuki' intention of three divorces is not valid because these words indicate divorce by way of iqtida', not by way of language. Because linguistically, 'Anti taliq' only indicates that the woman is described with divorce, but it does not indicate the proof of divorce by way of creation from the speaker. That is a shar'i matter not established linguistically. Because a woman being described with divorce is shar'i dependent on the husband divorcing his wife; so this is established by iqtida', and in iqtida' there is no generality, rather it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and the necessary extent is one divorce. An objection: that divorce which is established from the speaker by way of creation cannot be established by iqtida' because iqtida' in usul terminology is called that necessity which is required for the correctness of speech, and this necessity is antecedent. 'Thumma la' [؟] here it is not so, because the proof of divorce occurs with the word 'Anti taliq' itself, so its establishment is subsequent, not antecedent. So it is from the category of 'ibarah al-nass (explicit meaning), like saying 'Anti taliq talaqan' or 'Tallaqtuki talaqan'. Therefore intention of three is valid in it. We said: There are two responses to this objection. The first: that what is meant by the Lawgiver's placing these words for creation is not that the Lawgiver has completely removed the meaning of informing from these words and made their placement directly for creating and effecting divorce, and that this is their true meaning. Rather, the Lawgiver in all His placements maintains the consideration of linguistic placements. The Lawgiver chose words that indicate the establishment of meanings at present, such as past tense words and those specific to the present. When he says 'Anti taliq', this word is placed in language for informing – that is, it informs that the woman is divorced. For its correctness, it is necessary that the woman be described with divorce at present. To establish this description, the Lawgiver establishes by iqtida' the creation from the speaker so that the speech becomes correct; otherwise it would be a lie as an informatory statement. Therefore the proof of divorce is by iqtida'. This is what is meant by shar'i placement for creation. And when divorce is established by iqtida', intention of three is not valid because there is no generality for iqtida'. And a second reason: intention of three cannot be valid by way of metaphor because a group of three is a single conceptual unit, and intention of metaphor is valid in a word, as happens in specifying a word. Here in iqtida' there is no word; rather the meaning is considered. Therefore intending metaphor is also not valid. The second response: the word 'Anti taliq' linguistically indicates that divorce which is a quality of the woman, because the possessor of divorce and being described with divorce is the woman. Whereas that which is a quality of the man is tatliq (giving divorce). 'Anti taliq' does not indicate this linguistically; rather it indicates it by iqtida'. That divorce which is the quality of the woman and the linguistic meaning – in it intention of three is not valid because being described with divorce in itself is not multiple, because it is a single quality and attribute. Multiplicity is only in tatliq which is the husband's act, and with its multiplicity its necessary consequent.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the usul concept of iqtida' (implication) applied to the divorce phrase 'Anti taliq'. The author first compares 'Anti ba'in' to 'Anti taliq', noting that both establish a shar'i effect by iqtida', but in 'Anti ba'in' intention of three is valid because there are multiple types of banunah, whereas in 'Anti taliq' there is no multiplicity of types, only number. The passage then distinguishes iqtida' from mahzuf (ellipsis): in mahzuf, the omitted word's inclusion changes the grammatical structure and allows generality; in iqtida', the implied meaning does not change the uttered words and does not carry generality. This is illustrated with the Quranic example 'Wa is'al al-qaryah' where 'ahl' is omitted. The core ruling is that in 'Anti taliq', the proof of divorce is established by iqtida' because the linguistic meaning is merely informing that the woman is described with divorce; for the speech to be truthful and valid, the shar'i effect of divorce is necessarily implied. Since iqtida' only provides what is necessary for correctness, and one divorce suffices, intending three divorces is invalid. The author then presents an objection: if divorce is established directly by the utterance, it is from 'ibarah al-nass (explicit meaning), allowing intention of three. Two responses are given: (1) the shar'i placement does not negate the linguistic meaning; it uses the linguistic meaning to imply the creation of divorce, so it remains iqtida'; (2) even if metaphor were considered, it is invalid because iqtida' involves no word to specify. The final argument: the linguistic meaning of 'Anti taliq' is a single attribute (being divorced), which cannot be multiplied; multiplicity pertains only to the husband's act of talaq, which is not linguistically indicated.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): A necessary implication required for the correctness of speech; it does not alter the uttered words and carries no generality.
محذوف (mahzuf): An omitted word whose inclusion changes the grammatical structure and allows generality and particularity.
عبارة النص ('ibarah al-nass): Explicit meaning of the text derived directly from the wording without need for implication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in this passage, why is intention of three divorces invalid when saying 'Anti taliq'?
A) Because 'Anti taliq' is a metaphor and metaphor cannot be intended.
B) Because the word 'taliq' is a homonym for multiple meanings.
C) Because the proof of divorce is by iqtida', which does not carry generality and only provides what is necessary (one divorce).
D) Because the husband lacks authority to give three divorces in one utterance.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, iqtida' does not carry generality, so the phrase 'Anti taliq' effects only one divorce regardless of intention.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Intention of Triple Divorce in 'Anti Taliq': The Hanafi Distinction Between Attribute and Act
Usul analysis of niyyah in divorce wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of iqtida' to distinguish between the attribute meaning and the act meaning of 'talaq' in divorce pronouncements, and determine when intending multiple divorces is valid.

Core Idea
In 'anti taliq', the divorce is an attribute of the woman, singular; intending multiple is invalid unless the wording shifts to the husband's act (tatliq).

This lesson unpacks a classic Hanafi usul analysis: can a husband intend three divorces by simply saying 'anti taliq'? The answer hinges on whether the word 'talaq' in this phrase refers to the woman's attribute (sifat) or the husband's act of divorcing (tatliq). The commentator presents two answers, both rooted in the principle of iqtida' (implication), and distinguishes cases where the masdar is added. By the end, you will grasp how Hanafi usul resolves apparent conflicts between literal meaning and legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions of 'talaq'
Recognize that 'talaq' can denote the attribute of the woman (being divorced) or the act of the husband (giving divorce). The literal wording 'anti taliq' in Arabic primarily indicates the attribute.
This distinction is key to the entire analysis.
2
Understand the role of iqtida'
The word 'anti taliq' is literally informative (khabar), but for it to effect divorce, the Law creates the act of divorce by implication (iqtida'). This implied meaning is necessary for the utterance to be true.
Iqtida' is a Hanafi method of deriving a legal meaning that is not explicit but logically required.
3
Apply the distinction to niyyah
Since the literal meaning indicates a singular attribute, intending three divorces is invalid when the word stands alone. The attribute is one, not multiple.
Second answer in the text emphasizes this point.
4
Contrast with explicit mention of the masdar
When the husband says 'anti taliq talagan' or 'anti al-talaq', the masdar shifts the meaning to the act (tatliq), which can be multiple. Here, intending three is valid.
The first answer allows intending three even without the masdar if the word 'talaq' is uttered, but the second restricts it.
5
Analyze the two answers and their scope
The first answer holds that intending three is valid as long as the word 'talaq' is uttered, regardless of attribute/act distinction. The second answer restricts validity to cases where the masdar indicates the act. Both rely on iqtida' but differ in scope.
The text notes that the first answer applies to both 'anti taliq' and 'tallaqtuki', while the second only to 'anti taliq'.

ثابت کرے گی تاکہ یہ کلام صحیح ہو جائے ورنہ تو یہ بحیثیت اخبار کذب ہے لہذا ثبوت طلاق اقتضاء ہوگا یہی مراد ہوتی ہے انشاء کے لئے وضع شرعی کی واذا کان الطلاق ثابتا اقتضاء جب یہ طلاق اقتضا ثابت ہوئی تو نیۃ ثلث صحیح نہیں لانہ لا عموم للمقتضیٰ اور دوسری وجہ یہ بھی ہے کہ نیست ثلث بطریق مجانہ [؟] صحیح ہو سکتی بایں لحاظ کہ مجموعہ ثلث واحد اعتباری ہے اور نیت مجاز لفظ میں صحیح ہوتی ہے جیسے د تخصیص لفظ میں ہوا کرتی ہے اور یہاں اقتضا میں لفظ تو ہوتا نہیں ہے بلکہ معنی پیش نظر ہوتا ہے لہذا ارادہ مجاز بھی صحیح نہیں ہوگا ۔ وثانيهما به دوسرا جواب ہے حاصل یہ کہ لفظ انتِ طالق لغتہ اس طلاق پر دلالت کرتا ہے جو عورت کی صفت ہے کیونکہ ذی طلاق اور متصف بالطلاق عورت ہوتی ہے اور وہ جو صفہ رجل ہے وہ ہے تطلیق یعنی طلاق دینا انت طالق اس پر لغتہ دال نہیں البتہ اس پر اقتضاء دال ہے۔ نالذي هو صفة المرأة وہ طلاق جو عورت کی قوله فان قيل ثبوت البينونة الخ یہ اشکال ہے اس پر جو کہا گیا ہے کہ انت طالق میں جو مصدر بطور انشاء کے متکلم سے ثابت ہوتی ہے وہ امر شرعی ہے لغوی نہیں ہے تو اس کا ثبوت بطور
In the correctness of speech, there is need, and this is a prior necessary. But the matter is not like that here because the proof of divorce is occurring with the very word 'anti taliq', so its proof will be posterior, not prior. It will not be from the category of 'ibarat al-nass'. It is like 'anti taliq talagan' or 'tallaqt talagan', so intending three is valid here. We said about it: two answers. One is that the intended meaning of the legislator's placement is not that the Law has completely removed the informative meaning from these words and placed them directly for creating divorce, and that these are their real meanings. Rather, the Law in all its placements maintains the linguistic placements. The Law chose for the creation of a contract words that indicate the proof of meanings at the moment, like past tense verbs and words specific to the present. So when he says 'anti taliq', this word 'anti taliq' is placed in language for informing, i.e., it informs that the woman is divorced. For its correctness, it is necessary that the woman be presently described with divorce. To prove this description, the Law will establish a creation of divorce from the speaker by way of implication (iqtida') so that this speech becomes correct; otherwise, it would be a lie as an informative statement. Therefore, the proof of divorce will be by implication. This is the intended meaning of 'placement by the Law for creation'. And when the divorce is proven by implication, intending three is not correct because there is no generality in what is implied (al-muqtada). And the second reason is that intending three by way of metaphor (majaz) can [not] be correct, because the sum of three is one conventional unit, and intention in a metaphorical word is correct, as it occurs in the specification of a word, but here in implication there is no word, only the meaning is considered, so intending metaphorically is also not correct. And the second answer: the word 'anti taliq' in language indicates the divorce that is an attribute of the woman, because the possessor of divorce and the one described with divorce is the woman, and that which is an attribute of the man is 'tatliq', i.e., giving divorce. 'Anti taliq' does not indicate that linguistically; rather, it indicates it by implication. So that which is the attribute of the woman and the linguistic meaning, intending three is not correct in it because the description of divorce in itself is not multiple, as it is one quality and state. Multiplicity is actually in 'tatliq', which is the husband's act, and with its multiplicity, its necessary consequence, i.e., the divorce which is the woman's attribute, becomes multiple secondarily and derivatively. When there is no original multiplicity in this attribute of the woman, then intending three is not correct in it. And the creation [of divorce] which is the husband's attribute, its proof is by implication, and in that too intending three is not correct because there is no generality in the implied. This is the reason mentioned in al-Hidayah. The first answer includes both 'anti taliq' and 'tallaqtuki', while the second is specific to 'anti taliq'. And when he says 'anti taliq talagan' or 'anti al-talaq', in both intending three is correct. The justification according to this second answer is difficult, because the second answer states that in the divorce which is the woman's attribute, intending three is not correct, and in 'anti taliq talagan' undoubtedly that divorce is the woman's attribute, so intending three should not be correct. We say: when he intends three, it becomes specific that here the meaning of 'talaq' is 'tatliq', which is the husband's act, and it becomes a masdar. The implied phrase is 'anti taliq li-anni tallaqtuki tatlīqātin thalāthan'. And in his saying 'anti al-talaq', when he intends three, its meaning becomes 'you are the one upon whom three tatlīqāt have occurred'. As for the first answer, this objection does not arise because it does not say that intending three is not correct in the divorce that is the woman's attribute; rather, intending three is permissible in it, provided the word 'talaq' is uttered; intending three is correct even if it is the woman's attribute. And his saying 'like other generic nouns', i.e., when the word 'talaq' is in the position of being uttered, but since it is a generic noun, and a generic noun is a singular noun that does not indicate number but indicates the individual, whether that individual is real, like a real single, or a conventional individual, like all generic nouns: when they are uttered, they do not indicate number but indicate one, whether really one or conventionally, as will come in the chapter. The word 'al-talaq' is a noun for an individual; it includes a real single and the intention of a conventional single is also possible, and the sum as a whole is one conventional unit, and the sum as a whole in divorce is three, so intending it is also correct. His saying: 'For if it is said: the proof of separation...' This is an objection to what has been said that in 'anti taliq' the masdar that is established from the speaker by way of creation is a legal matter, not a linguistic one. So its proof is by way of...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis of the validity of intending triple divorce (niyyat al-thalath) when using the phrase 'anti taliq' versus 'anti taliq talagan'. The commentator, following the Hanafi school, presents two answers to a potential objection. The first answer emphasizes that the shar'i placement of words does not abolish their original linguistic meaning; rather, it relies on it. The word 'anti taliq' in language is informative (khabar), but for it to be valid as a divorce, the sharī'ah creates the act of divorce by way of implication (iqtida') to make the description true. In this implied sense, intending three is invalid because implication has no generality. The second answer distinguishes between the divorce that is an attribute of the woman (sifat al-mar'ah) and the act of the husband (tatliq). Since 'anti taliq' linguistically indicates the attribute, which is singular, intending multiple is not valid. However, if the masdar is added, as in 'anti taliq talagan', the word 'talagan' redirects the meaning to the act (tatliq), and then intending three becomes valid. The commentator also discusses the difference between the two answers, noting that the first answer allows intending three even in the attribute if the word 'talaq' is explicitly uttered, while the second answer restricts it. Finally, he explains that generic nouns like 'al-talaq' can indicate a conventional whole, making triple intention valid.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): ثبوت الشيء بطريق التضمن أو اللزوم العقلي، لا باللفظ الصريح.
صفة المرأة (sifat al-mar'ah): الطلاق الذي هو وصف في المرأة، وهو كونھا مطلقة، وهو واحد لا يتعدد.
تطلیق (tatliq): فعل الزوج، وهو إيقاع الطلاق، ويتعدد حقيقة.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer in the text, why is intending three divorces invalid in 'anti taliq'?
A) Because the word 'talaq' in it is a generic noun that does not indicate number.
B) Because 'anti taliq' linguistically indicates the attribute of the woman, which is a single quality.
C) Because the sharī'ah has placed the word for creation, not for information.
D) Because there is no generality in what is implied.

Key Takeaway
The validity of intending multiple divorces in 'anti taliq' depends on whether the wording indicates the woman's attribute (singular) or the husband's act (multiple), with iqtida' bridging the literal and legal meanings.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Iqtida' and Intention: Why Type Specifies but Number Does Not
Usul al-fiqh analysis of iqtida' with divorce examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that iqtida' permits intention for mutually exclusive types but not for numbers, using the example of divorce phrases.

Core Idea
Iqtida' proves only the minimal necessary — types are exclusive so intention works; numbers are not, so intention fails.

In this passage, the author explores how iqtida' (implication by necessity) operates differently when the implied meaning has exclusive types versus when it has varying numbers. The key example is the phrase 'anti ba'in' versus 'anti taliq taliqan'. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly deriving rulings in divorce and for applying Hanafi usul principles about generality and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implied meaning
In the phrase 'anti ba'in', the word 'ba'in' does not explicitly state talaq, but by iqtida', separation (baynuna) is necessarily implied because the utterance valid only if it refers to divorce.
Iqtida' is a type of dalala that proves the minimal necessary.
2
Recognize baynuna as a genus with species
Baynuna has two species: khafīfa (light, removable, i.e., one or two talags) and ghalīza (heavy, irreparable, i.e., three talags). They are mutually exclusive.
The combination of both species is impossible; only one can be meant.
3
Apply the rule of intention for types
Since iqtida' proves the genus but cannot specify which species is intended (both are equally minimal), the speaker's intention (niyya) is necessary and valid to determine the species.
If the speaker intends ghalīza, three talags occur; if khafīfa, one or two.
4
Contrast with talaq and numbers
For the word 'talaq', there is no type distinction; the difference is only by number (one, two, three). Numbers are not mutually exclusive but are multiple individuals. Iqtida' has no generality (umum), so it cannot prove a specific number beyond the minimal certain.
A 'certain minimal' exists for numbers (one talaq) but intention for a specific number like three is not compelled by necessity.
5
Derive the final ruling
Conclude: In iqtida', intention is valid for specifying mutually exclusive types because no minimal is certain; intention is invalid for specifying a number because the minimal (one) is sufficient and necessity does not require more.
This distinction is based on the nature of the implied meaning.

اگر اس طلاق واحدہ رجعیہ کے بعد اگر دو طلاق اور دیگا تو مغلظہ مطلقہ بطلاق ثلثہ ہو جائیگی
It would be by way of iqtida', so in this [case] specifying three [talaqs] is not correct. Likewise, the proof of baynuna... similarly in 'anti ba'in', the proof of baynuna from the speaker is a shari matter, therefore within it, specifying three is not correct. We say: yes, but baynuna is the answer. The reason for the answer is that we admit that baynuna is proven by way of iqtida', but baynuna, considered as baynuna, is common between the light and heavy [types]. The light is that which can be removed, and the heavy is that which cannot be removed, and that is three [talaqs]—these are the three talags. Or to say it as a genus: baynuna is one genus, and light and heavy are two species that fall under this genus. Intention of one of the two possible meanings: if baynuna is common, then intending one of the two possible meanings is valid in iqtida'. Similarly, intention of one of the two species: if baynuna is a genus, then intending one of the two species is valid in iqtida'. Because it is necessary that one of the two types be proven, and their combination is impossible; therefore it is necessary that the speaker intend one of them. But intending a specific number in iqtida' is not valid, because iqtida' has no generality—it does not indicate individuals at all, because iqtida' is proven on the basis of necessity, and there is no necessity for a specific number; only as much is proven as is necessary for the necessity to be removed, and that is the least amount that is certain. However, in the case of two types, there is no certain least amount, because types are mutually contradictory; thus there cannot be a certain least or most. Unlike few and many, where there is no type-based contradiction, so a certain least can exist. Therefore, intention of one of the two types is necessary; in the case of two types, whatever type the speaker intends is valid. If he intends heavy baynuna, then three talags are meant. And similarly, intending a metaphor is not valid in iqtida'. Therefore, in 'anti taliq taliqan', as we mentioned, intending three talags based on them being a single virtual unit is not valid because it is a metaphorical singular and cannot be intended in iqtida'. His statement: 'And not likewise talaq'—we said that the word talaq is not like the word baynuna, because the individuals of talaq differ not by type but only by number: one, two, or three talags. So this is difference by number, not by type... And it cannot be said that talaq is divided into types. [A question:] The summary is that just as baynuna has two types—one that can be removed and the other that cannot—similarly talaq is also of two kinds. ... But talaq is not removed: one talaq has left the husband's possession, he owns two remaining talags. With revocation, ownership of three talags does not return; if after one raj'i talaq he gives two more, he becomes a permanently divorced person by three talags.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of how iqtida' (implication by necessity) works in the context of divorce. The discussion centers on the phrase 'anti ba'in' (you are separated). The Hanafi position is that in such a phrase, baynuna (separation) is proven by iqtida', but the specific number or type of separation is not automatically determined. However, baynuna can be either khafīfa (light, removable, i.e., one or two talags) or ghalīza (heavy, irreparable, i.e., three talags). Since these are two mutually exclusive types, the speaker's intention (niyya) is valid to specify which type he meant. If he intends the heavy type, then three talags are established. In contrast, for the word 'talāq' itself, there is no such type distinction; talāq is a single entity that, once issued, removes the marriage bond to that extent. Even raj'i talaq is still a talaq that cannot be 'removed' in the sense that the count remains. Therefore, intending a specific number in iqtida' for talāq is not valid because numbers are multiple individuals, and iqtida' only proves the minimal necessary. The author emphasizes that iqtida' has no generality (umum), so specifying a number like three is not compelled by necessity. This is a classic Hanafi usul argument distinguishing between type (type) and number in implied meanings.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Implication by necessity: a linguistic meaning that is necessarily required for the utterance to be valid, even if not explicitly stated.
بینونت (baynuna): Separation; in divorce context, the state of being separated from one's spouse.
نیة (niyya): Intention of the speaker to specify which meaning is intended when the word is ambiguous between types or species.
مشترک (mushtarak): A word that has more than one meaning and is equally likely for each (homonym).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is intention to specify a number (e.g., three) invalid in iqtida' for talaq, but intention to specify a type (heavy baynuna) is valid?
A) Because talaq has no generality; baynuna has generality.
B) Because talaq is a genus; baynuna is not.
C) Because iqtida' has no generality, so it cannot prove a specific number; types are mutually exclusive so intention resolves ambiguity.
D) Because numbers are not part of divine law; types are.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, iqtida' (implication by necessity) permits intention to specify mutually exclusive types (like light vs. heavy baynuna) but does not permit intention to specify a particular number (like three talags) because iqtida' has no generality and only the minimal necessary is proven.

Conditions for the Validity of Mafhum al-Mukhalafah
Explains definition and conditions for validity of counter-indicative meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six conditions under which mafhum al-mukhalafah is accepted as a proof and apply them to textual examples.

Core Idea
A ruling's opposite meaning can only be inferred if specific context conditions are met.

In usul al-fiqh, mafhum al-mukhalafah (counter-indicative meaning) is a contested method. The Shafi'i school accepts it with strict conditions, while the Hanafi school generally rejects it. This passage outlines the conditions that proponents require for mafhum to be valid. Understanding these conditions helps you analyze how different schools treat textual implications and avoid misapplying an inference that the text does not support.

Guided Path
1
Define the Core Terms
Understand mantuq (stated ruling), maskut 'anhu (unmentioned case), and mafhum mukhalafah (opposite inference). The passage defines mafhum as establishing the opposite ruling for the unmentioned case.
Mafhum is distinct from mantuq and dalalah al-nass.
2
Identify the Priority/Equality Condition
If the unmentioned case is more deserving of the ruling (priority) or equal to the stated case, the ruling is proven by dalalah al-nass or qiyas, not by mafhum. Mafhum only operates when the unmentioned case is less deserving.
This condition prevents mafhum from overriding stronger indications.
3
Apply the Custom Exception
If the stated condition is customary (ghalib) – like 'stepdaughters in your laps' – then mafhum is invalid because the condition is not meant to be exclusive.
Customary conditions are often attached to rulings without implying limitation.
4
Check for Negation and Context
The text must indicate negation of the ruling for the unmentioned case. Additionally, if the statement is in response to a question or incident, or if the speaker knows the listener is ignorant of the specific ruling, mafhum does not apply.
These context conditions ensure the text's specificity is preserved.
5
Apply Conditions to Examples
Practice with the grazing camels example: the hadith 'In grazing camels is zakat' was given in response to a question, so no mafhum is derived. Similarly, the stepdaughters example shows custom blocks mafhum.
These examples illustrate how conditions interact in real texts.

Source Text

ساتھ خصوص صفہ ذکر کرتا ہے کہا اذا علم جیسے متکلم جانتا ہے کہ ان السامع یعنی سامع ابل سائمہ میں زکوۃ کے وجوب کو نہیں جانتا اس بنا پر متکلم نے کہا ان فی الابل السائمہ زکوۃ تو یہ بھی دلالت نہیں کرتا کہ عدم سوم کی وجہ سے وجوب زکوۃ منعدم ہو جائیگا
Prohibition of hitting and reviling, and if the ruling of the unmentioned is opposite to the ruling of the stated, it is called counter-indicative meaning (mafhum mukhalafah). They derive proof from this mafhum mukhalafah and establish the ruling contrary to the stated in the unmentioned. But for it they set several conditions, mentioning them: the condition of mafhum al-mukhalafah according to those who accept it: that no priority appears, nor equality; even if priority appears, the ruling is established in the unmentioned by the text's indication (dalalah al-nass) in case of priority, or by analogy in case of equality; that the stated is not expressed according to custom; that it does not indicate negation of the ruling; that the stated is not in response to a question or incident; and that the speaker does not know that the listener is ignorant of the specific ruling — like saying 'indeed in grazing camels is zakat' does not indicate that non-grazing camels have no zakat.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage defines mafhum al-mukhalafah (counter-indicative meaning) and lists its conditions according to those who accept it (mainly Shafi'is). The Hanafi position is not directly stated here but implied by the conditions being those of the proponents. The text explains that when the unmentioned case has priority or equality in the ruling, the text itself or analogy applies, not mafhum. If the stated is customary, like 'your stepdaughters in your laps', the condition is customary, so mafhum is not valid. Also, if the stated negates the ruling for others, it must do so explicitly; if it is in response to a question or incident, mafhum is invalid. Lastly, if the speaker mentions a specific condition because the listener is ignorant, mafhum does not apply. These conditions restrict the application of mafhum mukhalafah.

Key Terms
مفہوم مخالفتہ (mafhum mukhalafah): The counter-indicative meaning derived from a text, implying the opposite ruling for the unmentioned case.
منطوق (mantuq): The explicitly stated ruling or subject in the text.
مسکوت عنہ (maskut 'anhu): The unmentioned case that may be inferred from the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It remains valid as long as other conditions are met.
B) It becomes invalid because the specific context precludes general inference.
C) It is always valid because any answer implies opposites.
D) It is only invalid if the question was about grazing camels.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum al-Mukhalafah requires strict conditions; it is not an automatic inference from any text.

Takhsis al-Shay' bi-Ismihi and Hanafi Rejection
Presents and refutes the view that specifying by name creates counter-indicative meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects specification by name (takhsis al-shay' bi-ismihi) as evidence for mafhum mukhalafah and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Specification by name does NOT imply the opposite ruling for unnamed entities.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: whether specifying an object by its name in a ruling creates a counter-indicative meaning (mafhum mukhalafah). The Shafi'i school, drawing on the Ansar's understanding of a hadith, affirms this. The Hanafi school rejects it with powerful logical and consensus-based arguments. We will unpack the reasoning and see why the Hanafi position carries the day.

Guided Path
1
Understand mafhum mukhalafah
Recall that mafhum mukhalafah is the implied opposite ruling for unmentioned cases. This lesson focuses on one type: specification by name (takhsis al-shay' bi-ismihi). Grasp the Shafi'i claim that naming an object as the subject of a ruling implies the negation of that ruling for all other objects.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Study the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'i' and the Ansar's inference. The Shafi'is argue that the Prophet attached ghusl to 'ma' (water/mani), so by mafhum mukhalafah, other substances do not obligate ghusl.
This hadith is ahad and its dalala is zanni.
3
Analyze the Hanafi reductio ad absurdum
The Hanafis show that if specification by name indicated mafhum mukhalafah, then the statement 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' would deny prophethood to all others (falsehood and disbelief), and 'Zaid mawjud' would negate the existence of Allah and other prophets (also false and disbelief). This proves the absurdity of the principle.
The author prioritizes the most evident consequence: in the first example disbelief is foremost; in the second, falsehood.
4
Identify the second Hanafi proof: consensus on qiyas
The Hanafis argue that if specification by name created mafhum mukhalafah, it would contradict the consensus (ijma') of scholars that the specified ruling can be extended via qiyas based on the common effective cause (ta'lil). The fact that qiyas is valid shows that the ruling is not limited to the named entity.
This proof demonstrates that mafhum mukhalafah is not needed; qiyas already serves the purpose.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Synthesize: specification by name does not generate mafhum mukhalafah. The Hanafi school relies on qiyas to extend rulings, not on implied opposites. This protects the integrity of language and avoids logical contradictions.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Shafi'is may derive new rulings from such specifications; Hanafis use qiyas based on the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جب مفہوم مخالفہ کے شرائط بیان ہو چکے تو اب اس کے اقسام کا ذکر کیا فقال منه یعنی مفہوم مخالفت میں سے ایک یہ مسئلہ ہے ۔ ان تخصیص الشئ باسمه یعنی اگر ایک حکم کی شئی پر اس کے مخصوص اسم پر کیا جائے خواہ وہ اسم جنس ہو یا اسم علم بدل علی نقی الحکم عما عداہ اس شئی کے ماسوی سے حکم کے انتفا پر یہ تخصیص اسمی دال ہو گی۔ عند البعض نزدیک بعض حضرات کے یعنی عند الشوافع لان الانصار فهموا من قوله عليه السلام الماء من الماء پہلے الماء سے مراد غسل ہے اور دوسرے الماء سے منی ہے معنی یہ ہوا الغسل من المنی تو رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے قتل کا حکم منی کے اسم جنس الماء پر لگایا ہے تو انصار نے اس سے یہی سمجھا کہ اکسال پر غسل واجب نہیں۔ دھو ای ال اکسال یہ ہے کہ ذکر قبل الانزال سست اور ڈھیلا ہو جائے اور انزال منی کی نوبت نہ آئے تو یہی مفہوم مخالفہ سے استدلال ہے کہ جہاں انزال مار نہ ہو وہاں غسل واجب نہیں ہے۔ وعندنا لا یدل یعنی ہمارے نزدیک یہ تخصیص مفهوم مخالف پر نہ دال ہے نہ اس کی دلیل ہے ۔ والايلزم الكفر والكذب في محمد رسول الله وزيد موجود یعنی اگر اسمی تخصیص مفہوم مخالفة کو ثابت کرتی ہے تو ان دونوں مثالوں میں کفر اور کذب لازم آتا ہے کیونکہ پہلی مثال میں بطور مفہوم مخالفہ کے یہ لازم آتا ہے محمد صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے علاوہ کوئی شخص رسول نہیں ہے ۔ یہ کفر بھی ہے اور کذب بھی کیونکہ سیدنا محمد صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے علاوہ بہت سے حضرات رسول ہوئے ہیں۔ اور زید موجود میں زید کے علاوہ تمام موجودات کے وجود کی نفی ہوگی تو اس میں وجود باری تعالیٰ اور وجود رسل علیہم السلام کی نفی ہو جاتی ہے جو کذب بھی ہے اور کفر بھی ۔ مصنف نے اول میں اظہر اللوازم اور اقوی کو پیش نظر رکھا اس لئے اول میں کفر کا نام لیا اگرچہ کذب بھی اس کے اندر لازم آتا ہے ۔ ثانی میں کذب اظہر ہے اگر چہ کفر بھی ساتھ لازم آتا ہے لیکن خیلی بات کو پیش نظر رکھتے ہوئے کذب کے ذکر پر اکتفا کیا۔ ان امثلہ سے معلوم ہوا کہ تخصیص اسمی سے مفہوم مخالفة ثابت نہیں ہوتا۔ ولاجماع العلماء على جواز التحلیل یعنی تخصیص اسمی سے مفہوم مخالفہ ثابت کرنا اس اجماع کے منافی ہے کہ ان تخصیصات میں تعلیل النص صحیح ہے پھر منصوص کے ساتھ اس علتہ میں جو مشارک ہو حکم منصوص اس مشارک میں بطور قیاس کے صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ قیاس اسی کا نام ہے کہ فرع میں حکم اصل کو بوجہ علہ مشارکہ کے ثابت کیا جاتا ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ جو حکم اصل میں ہے وہ اس کے ماحدا میں حکم
When the conditions of mafhum mukhalafah have been stated, now mention is made of its types: from it is this issue: that specifying a thing by its name — whether a generic name or a proper name — indicates the opposite ruling for other than that thing according to some (i.e. Shafi'is). Because the Ansar understood from his saying 'al-ma'u min al-ma'i' that the first 'ma' means ghusl and the second 'ma' means mani, so the meaning is 'ghusl from mani'. The Prophet attached the ruling of ghusl to the generic name 'ma', so the Ansar understood that ghusl is not obligatory for iksal (sexual excitement without emission). This is inference by mafhum mukhalafah: where there is no emission, ghusl is not obligatory. According to us (Hanafis), this specification does not indicate mafhum mukhalafah, nor is it evidence for it. It would necessitate disbelief and falsehood in 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' and 'Zaid mawjud' because if specification by name indicates mafhum mukhalafah, then 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' would imply no one else is a messenger (disbelief and falsehood) and 'Zaid is present' would negate the existence of Allah and other messengers (falsehood and disbelief). The author prioritized the clearer consequence. Also, it contradicts the consensus of scholars on the validity of reasoning (ta'lil) with these specifications, because analogy (qiyas) is valid based on the common effective cause. Thus the ruling in the original applies to other cases through qiyas, showing that specification by name does not establish mafhum mukhalafah.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage discusses the first type of mafhum mukhalafah mentioned by the author: specification by name (takhsis al-shay' bi-ismihi). The Shafi'i view, as exemplified by the Ansar's understanding of the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'i', holds that when the Prophet specified ghusl with the word 'ma' (water/mani), it implies that ghusl is not required for any other substance. The Hanafi rebuttal is twofold: first, if this logic were applied universally, it would lead to absurd conclusions like denying other prophets and even Allah's existence when saying 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' or 'Zaid is present'. Second, it contradicts the consensus that analogy (qiyas) can extend rulings based on a common cause; the original text's ruling can be applied to other cases through qiyas, not through mafhum. Therefore, specification by name does not create a counter-indicative meaning. This is a classic usul dispute where Hanafis reject mafhum mukhalafah as a proof, while Shafi'is accept it with conditions.

Key Terms
تخصیص الشئ باسمه (takhsis al-shay' bi-ismihi): Specifying an object by its name (generic or proper) in a ruling, which some claim creates a counter-indicative meaning.
مفہوم مخالفتہ (mafhum mukhalafah): The implied opposite ruling for unmentioned cases derived from a text's specific wording.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does the Hanafi argument derive from accepting specification by name as indicating mafhum mukhalafah?
A) It would make all names meaningless.
B) It would lead to falsehood and disbelief when using proper names like 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' or 'Zaid is present'.
C) It would contradict the Qur'an.
D) It would nullify all analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Specification by name does not create a counter-indicative meaning; rather, the ruling's scope is determined by the underlying cause, allowing analogical extension via qiyas.

Takhsis bi al-Ism and Mafhum al-Mukhalafah: The Hanafi Rejection
Explaining the Hanafi rejection of specification by name as proof for mafhum al-mukhalafah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis reject takhsis bi al-ism as proof for mafhum al-mukhalafah and apply the Hanafi reasoning to refute the Shafi'i argument.

Core Idea
Specifying a ruling by a name does not imply its opposite for all else; otherwise absurdities like denying other prophets arise.

In this passage, the author introduces the first type of mafhum al-mukhalafah: specification by name (takhsis bi al-ism). The Shafi'is consider such specification as indicating the negation of the ruling for everything not mentioned. Hanafis vehemently reject this, providing logical refutations and an alternative interpretation of the primary Shafi'i proof, the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma''. Let's walk through the Hanafi critique and understand why the Hanafi school limits the scope of mafhum al-mukhalafah.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'is claim that when a ruling is attached to a thing by its name (generic or proper), it implies the opposite ruling for all other cases. They rely on the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' understood as 'ghusl is from mani', implying no ghusl without mani.
2
Identify the Hanafi refutation with examples
Hanafis counter with two examples: 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' would then imply no other prophet, which is kufr and false; 'Zaid mawjud' would negate existence of everything else including Allah.
The author presents the most extreme consequence to show the absurdity.
3
Trace the argument from consensus and qiyas
If specification by name implied negation, then ta'lil (reasoning by 'illah) and qiyas would be invalid, contradicting scholarly consensus. The text would negate the ruling for all other cases, blocking any extension by qiyas.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the hadith
Hanafis explain that the Ansar's understanding was not based on mafhum al-mukhalafah but on the lam of istighraq in 'al-ma'u', meaning 'every ghusl is from mani'. The meeting of circumcised parts is a legal indication of ejaculation, so ghusl is obligatory upon it, similar to travel as indicator for shortening salah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Shafi'is may require direct evidence of mani for ghusl; Hanafis deem ghusl obligatory upon meeting of circumcised parts, regardless of actual ejaculation.

He does not know obligation. On this basis, the mutakallim said: 'Indeed, in camels that are grazing, zakat is due.' This does not indicate that due to the absence of grazing, the obligation of zakat is negated. When the conditions of mafhum al-mukhalafah have been explained, now its types are mentioned. So he said: 'Minhu' i.e., from mafhum al-mukhalafah is this issue: that specification of a thing by its name (takhsis al-shay' bi-ismihi) means that if a ruling is made on a thing by its specific name, whether it is a generic name or a proper name, it indicates the negation of the ruling from everything else. According to some, i.e., according to the Shafi'is, because the Ansar understood from his statement, peace be upon him, 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' , that the first 'al-ma'' refers to ghusl and the second 'al-ma'' refers to mani. The meaning is: 'al-ghusl min al-mani'. So the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, placed the ruling of [ghusl] on the generic name 'al-ma' for mani. The Ansar understood from this that ghusl is not obligatory upon 'al-iksal' (something). 'Dho al-iksal' is that the penis becomes limp before ejaculation and ejaculation does not occur. So this is reasoning by mafhum al-mukhalafah that where ejaculation does not occur, ghusl is not obligatory. And according to us, it does not indicate; i.e., in our view, this specification is not indicative of mafhum al-mukhalafah nor is it its proof. And [if it did indicate], it would necessitate disbelief and lying in [the phrases] 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' and 'Zaid mawjud'. Because in the first example, by way of mafhum al-mukhalafah, it would necessitate that no person besides Muhammad, peace be upon him, is a messenger. That is both disbelief and lying, because besides our master Muhammad, peace be upon him, many messengers have come. And in 'Zaid mawjud', the existence of all existents besides Zaid would be negated, which would negate the existence of Allah Almighty and the existence of the messengers, peace be upon them, which is both lying and disbelief. The author first considered the most apparent and strongest implications, so he mentioned disbelief first, although lying is also implied. In the second, lying is more apparent, although disbelief is also implied, but considering brevity, he sufficed with mentioning lying. From these examples, it is known that specification by name does not prove mafhum al-mukhalafah. And [it contradicts] the consensus of scholars on the permissibility of ta'lil (rationale), because proving mafhum al-mukhalafah by specification by name contradicts this consensus that ta'lil of the text is valid in these specifications. Then, along with the text, whatever shares that 'illah, the ruling of the text is valid for that participant by way of qiyas. Because qiyas is named so because the ruling of the original is proven in the branch due to shared 'illah. So it is known that the ruling that is in the original does not indicate a contradictory ruling for the other. Because if mafhum al-mukhalafah were valid, then the text would negate by way of mafhum al-mukhalafah, and then establishing the ruling via qiyas for those other than the text would not be valid, because that qiyas has no validity when it contradicts the text. And their understanding that from 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' is the response. The summary is that the Ansar's understanding of the non-obligation of ghusl was not due to mafhum al-mukhalafah, but because the first word 'al-masar' which means ghusl has the lam of istighraq, meaning every instance of ghusl of janabah is obligatory from mani. So they thought that when mani is not apparent, ghusl does not occur. So 'ghayr in al-ma'' answers an objection. The objection is: when the lam is for istighraq, then the meaning would be that all instances of ghusl of janabah occur only when there is existence of mani. Then when there is meeting of the two circumcised parts but no mani, then ghusl should not be obligatory? So the answer is that ghusl without water (i.e., without mani) is not proven, but existence of mani is sometimes proven by sight and sometimes by indication, and meeting of the two circumcised parts is evidence of ejaculation, and ejaculation is a subtle matter. Therefore, the ruling is established with the evidence of ejaculation, and the evidence of ejaculation is meeting of the two circumcised parts. Wherever this evidence is present, ghusl becomes obligatory. Like the concession of ease, such as the concession of shortening prayer and breaking fast based on hardship, so the evidence of hardship was taken as travel; wherever travel exists, this concession is established, whether actual hardship is felt or not.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a discussion on the types of mafhum al-mukhalafah (implication of the contrary) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first states that having explained the conditions of mafhum al-mukhalafah, he now mentions its types. The first type is 'takhsis al-shay' bi ismihi' (specification of a thing by its name). He defines it: when a ruling is attached to a thing by its specific name (whether generic or proper), according to some scholars (the Shafi'is), this indicates that the ruling is negated for everything else. The Shafi'is base this on the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'', which they interpret as 'ghusl is from mani'. The Ansar understood from this that if there is no mani (as in the case of madhi before ejaculation), ghusl is not obligatory. The Hanafi position ('indana') is that such specification does not indicate mafhum al-mukhalafah. The proof is that if it did, then phrases like 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' would imply that no other prophet exists, which is kufr and lies. Similarly, 'Zaid mawjud' would negate the existence of everything else, including Allah and other prophets. Moreover, accepting mafhum al-mukhalafah from such specification would contradict the consensus that ta'lil (reasoning by 'illah) is permissible. If the text's ruling is considered to negate all others by implication, then qiyas (analogy) on the basis of a shared 'illah would be invalid because it would contradict the text's implied negation. The author then addresses the Ansar's inference from the hadith, explaining that their understanding was not based on mafhum al-mukhalafah but on the lam of istighraq (comprehensive lam) in 'al-ma'' meaning 'every ghusl'. They thought ghusl is only required when mani is present, but the Hanafi response is that while ghusl is not proven without direct sight of mani, the meeting of circumcised parts is a legal indication (dalil) of ejaculation, which is a subtle matter. Therefore, ghusl becomes obligatory wherever this indication is found, similar to how the concession of shortening prayer is attached to travel, whether actual hardship is felt or not, because travel is the legal cause.

Key Terms
مفہوم مخالفہ (mafhum al-mukhalafah): The implication of the contrary; a ruling that is understood to be negated for what is not mentioned.
تخصیص اسمی (takhsis ismi): Specification of a thing by its name, whether generic or proper, to indicate the ruling is confined to that named thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following is NOT a reason to reject takhsis bi al-ism as proof for mafhum al-mukhalafah?
A) It would lead to kufr in phrases like 'Muhammad Rasul Allah'.
B) It contradicts the consensus on the permissibility of ta'lil and qiyas.
C) The hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' is weak in transmission.
D) The Ansar's understanding was based on lam istighraq, not mafhum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects specification by name as a valid indicator of mafhum al-mukhalafah, relying on logical consistency and the preservation of qiyas.

Specification by Description as Mafhum al-Mukhalafah: Shafi'i Position
Explains specification by description as a type of mafhum al-mukhalafah according to the Shafi'is

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shafi'i claim that takhsis bi al-wasf implies negation of the ruling for other cases, and identify how the verse 'min fatayātikumu al-mu'mināt' is used as evidence.

Core Idea
When a ruling is attached to a description, Shafi'is infer the opposite for all that lacks that description.

Mafhum al-mukhalafah (opposite understanding) comes in several types. In this lesson, we examine the second type: specification by description (takhsis bi al-wasf). According to the Shafi'is, if a ruling is linked to a specific qualifier, it automatically negates that ruling for everything without that qualifier. The Hanafi school generally rejects this automatic implication, but the present passage only details the Shafi'i argument.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim
The text states that when a ruling specifies a thing by a description, it indicates the negation of the ruling from what lacks that description. This is the Shafi'i view.
The passage also provides a grammatical analysis of the author's words.
2
Identify the evidence
The Shafi'is use the Quranic verse 'min fatayātikumu al-mu'mināt' (from your believing slave girls) as an example. The description 'believing' restricts the permissible marriage to believing slave girls.
Verse 4:25 from Surah al-Nisa'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Shafi'is argue that by specifying 'believing', the verse implies that the opposite case (non-believing slave girls) is not allowed. Thus, marriage with them is invalid.
This is the core of the usul argument.
4
Note the dispute
The Hanafi school does not accept that a description alone suffices for mafhum al-mukhalafah. They require additional contextual evidence (qara'in) to establish such an implication.
The full Hanafi response is not given in this passage.
